<div id="review_2150242779" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2150242779" />
    <a title="Magdalen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/46843838-magdalen"><img alt="Magdalen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581146923p2/46843838.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2150242779?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Magdalen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Magdalen" href="/user/show/46843838-magdalen">Magdalen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143106036" href="/book/show/7094813-kokoro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46843838-magdalen?shelf=japan">japan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2150242779" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11094863440266038824">Kokoro is the story of an unnamed protagonist who meets his mentor whom he refers to as Sensei. Truth be told not much take place in the book as far as action is concerned so if you are looking for a page turner you'll probably end up disappointed. However, Soseki manages to portray a whole era masterfully and he really unravels the depths of human psyche. The themes included in the book are mistrusting people, dysfunctional communication, inability to relate to other people's feelings, sadness,</span>
  <span id="freeText11094863440266038824" style="display:none">Kokoro is the story of an unnamed protagonist who meets his mentor whom he refers to as Sensei. Truth be told not much take place in the book as far as action is concerned so if you are looking for a page turner you'll probably end up disappointed. However, Soseki manages to portray a whole era masterfully and he really unravels the depths of human psyche. The themes included in the book are mistrusting people, dysfunctional communication, inability to relate to other people's feelings, sadness, love and finally redemption (&amp; more i just pinpointed the ones i remember clearly) <br /><br />What is also great about Kokoro, other than the story, the atmosphere of the book and the characters, is the fact that the chapters are short and the language is easy to follow (the translation obviously) Soseki brilliantly captures your attention from the very first page. And as you get more into the story and as the story eventually unfolds you can't help but wonder. "What happened to Sensei?" "why is Sensei a misanthrope?" And once you reach the last page you know and you understand. <br /><br />Kokoro is definitely melancholic and sometimes depressing but it was a beautiful and a unique book. One thing is certain · i will be reading more works of Soseki in the near future. <br /><br />Ps: it really hits me how a book so simply written can have a huge impact on a person.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11094863440266038824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2150242779">

<div id="review_1805599298" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1805599298" />
    <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes"><img alt="Nancy Oakes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507555979p2/900340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1805599298?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy Oakes" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes">Nancy Oakes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143106036" href="/book/show/7094813-kokoro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=japanese-fiction">japanese-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=translated-fiction">translated-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=page-to-screen">page-to-screen</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1805599298" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18367058290983941806">11/30/2016 - update: thanks to my love for foreign films, British comedy and other shows which for some reason we cannot get here, we have multi-region dvd capability & so I was able to watch the movie based on this novel. Scroll to the bottom of this post if at all interested in the film.*<br/><br/><i>Kokoro</i> is,as I discovered, one of those novels where a second reading and a bit of research can completely change what you thought about it after the first time through. The second read was spurred by 1) disco</span>
  <span id="freeText18367058290983941806" style="display:none">11/30/2016 - update: thanks to my love for foreign films, British comedy and other shows which for some reason we cannot get here, we have multi-region dvd capability &amp; so I was able to watch the movie based on this novel. Scroll to the bottom of this post if at all interested in the film.*<br /><br /><i>Kokoro</i> is,as I discovered, one of those novels where a second reading and a bit of research can completely change what you thought about it after the first time through. The second read was spurred by 1) discovering that a scholarly controversy had arisen over this book and 2) deciding to pick up and read another translation along with an introduction that explained said controversy. After much time to focus, think and absorb, well, the second time around actually clarified things I had trouble zooming in on after the first time. <br /><br />The novel is structured in three parts. In part one, the narrator meets and attaches himself to an elder man he calls Sensei, and is soon "yearning for the possibilities of all he had to offer." However, Sensei, who reveals that he distrusts humanity, is reticent to open up about himself, and our narrator often finds himself frustrated when, as he says, "I failed to gain what I sought from him in matters of the mind." However, Sensei also reveals that while he is "suspicious" of most people, he realizes that the narrator seems "too straightforward and open for that," and that Sensei, before he dies, wants to "have trusted just one person." If the younger man could "be that person, ... sincerely in earnest," from his heart, then he will reveal to him the story of his past and leave nothing out, but not right away, since "It requires a suitable moment." What he wants in return is left unspoken for the moment. Eventually the narrator will learn all, but not before part two, which finds him back home with his parents. While there because his father's health is failing, he abandons his own family at a critical moment due to some disturbing news from Sensei, which sets up part three, where all is revealed. Sadly, since everything sort of turns on the revelations in part three, I have to keep silent, since to tell would be to spoil, but this is actually the part where we come to understand Sensei and where we learn exactly what it is he expects from the narrator -- it isn't expressly stated in so many words, but trust me, it's there. <br /><br />I wish I could make this post less cryptic, but there's a lot happening in this novel that a reader really needs to experience and sort out on his/her own. Look for thematic elements such as the formation of bonds, relationships, betrayal, individual vs. social responsibility, love, and above all, what it really means to bare one's soul/psyche to another. What I will say is that after the second reading, <i>Kokoro</i> became an even darker book than it was the first time through, which I didn't think was possible. <br /><br />Highly recommended for readers of Japanese literature; it probably won't take everyone two readings, but I got a lot more out of it by doing it that way. <br /><br />* the movie:<br /><br />Big differences between page and screen abound here, many of which are covered in the dvd liner notes. As just one example, it's easy to see just how the movie "simplifies" Soseki's novel, "reorders its plot and eliminates some of its subtext while playing up the homosexual implications that are merely latent in the original." I have to say that since the subject doesn't actually come up labeled as such in the novel, I was quite surprised to see a scene in this movie where Mrs. Sensei starts wondering out loud whether or not there's some sort of attraction between her husband and Hioki (the novel's narrator, given a name here) which Hioki quickly denies with an "it's not what your thinking!" response. On the other hand, there's a lot that's left out as well. <br /><br /> Much of the story is revealed through flashbacks, which I think is the best way to have done it, since so much of the novel turns on what happened in the past, which seems to be the overall point of the film. I've seen people criticize this approach used here, but I really don't see how else it could have been done. To tell it in a linear-narrative, chronological style would have wrecked things (as it would have in the novel as well). While the movie is certainly worth watching, the book is much, much better, and should definitely be read first. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18367058290983941806" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1808756963" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1808756963" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1808756963?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=2020-aty-challenge">2020-aty-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=kindle-purchase">kindle-purchase</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=asia">asia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1808756963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11568319566753660481">Some books I close the cover on and I could tell you that minute exactly what I think of them, what they were trying to convey, and whether they have done it well. This isn’t one of those books. I finished it. I sat a while. I pondered. I wondered even then if there was something about our narrator and Sensei’s relationship that I had missed, something about Sensei and his friend, K’s? Sensei means “teacher”, but what exactly did he wish to teach our young narrator and did he succeed? <br/><br/>I wondered</span>
  <span id="freeText11568319566753660481" style="display:none">Some books I close the cover on and I could tell you that minute exactly what I think of them, what they were trying to convey, and whether they have done it well. This isn’t one of those books. I finished it. I sat a while. I pondered. I wondered even then if there was something about our narrator and Sensei’s relationship that I had missed, something about Sensei and his friend, K’s? Sensei means “teacher”, but what exactly did he wish to teach our young narrator and did he succeed? <br /><br />I wondered if the main thrust of this novel might not be carpe diem. Our narrator is a procrastinator and seems to operate in a fog of not knowing what he wants or what he should do. He is very attracted to Sensei, who is a man who by his own definition does nothing. But Sensei is a man who has lost a great deal, perhaps his one chance at happiness, primarily because he, himself, failed to act or rather acted too late. I could not see that he had set our narrator on a better path than the one he had followed, in fact, I think he might be primarily responsible for a despicable action the narrator takes toward the end of the novel, a purely unforgivable one in my eyes.<br /><br />Soseki’s writing is lyrical and poetic, with an unmistakable Asian quality to it. I admired the way he wove the story around the lives of these two men about whom we knew actually so little in the end. There were a million things I would have liked to ask, but ultimately none of the answers would have mattered, Soseki has told us all we need to know.<br /><br /><i>The beautiful sky began slowly to lose its brightness. And before us, the delicate, green maple leaves, which looked like drops of water just about to fall from the branches, seemed to grow darker in color. From the road below, the sound of cart wheels reach our ears. I imagined that a man from the village had loaded his cart with plants or vegetables, and was on his way to some fair to sell them. Sensei stood up, as though the sound had roused him from his meditation.</i><br /><br />Perhaps this is also a novel about betrayal and how the hatred that follows it rots the soul. Perhaps we are meant to see betrayal from both sides, to see in Sensei both the betrayer and the betrayed. <br /><br />A full day later, I cannot seem to sort this book into any neat category. I did not love it, that is certain, but I did admire the writing and feel there was something very important Soseki wished to say that never fully penetrated my mind. It is not simple to get to “the heart of things”, which is what “kokoro” means, but I must applaud Soseki for trying. 3.5 rounded up for the sheer beauty of the language.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11568319566753660481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1962628041" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1962628041" />
    <a title="Siti Shakira Suhaimi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26991774-siti-shakira-suhaimi"><img alt="Siti Shakira Suhaimi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603204710p2/26991774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1962628041?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Siti Shakira Suhaimi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Siti Shakira Suhaimi" href="/user/show/26991774-siti-shakira-suhaimi">Siti Shakira Suhaimi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1962628041" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15216243973475276757">This amazing 248 pages Classics was written beautifully, enough to get my attachment after few pages. It told stories on human relationship with one another, on different degree. Relationship that happen to everyone. The feelings that probably can be called 'love' of different angle, of different literary meaning, and individual's actions based on the feeling. Sometimes we didn't notice we did something because of love, as we didn't even notice the love is there. We thought we are doing it to fi</span>
  <span id="freeText15216243973475276757" style="display:none">This amazing 248 pages Classics was written beautifully, enough to get my attachment after few pages. It told stories on human relationship with one another, on different degree. Relationship that happen to everyone. The feelings that probably can be called 'love' of different angle, of different literary meaning, and individual's actions based on the feeling. Sometimes we didn't notice we did something because of love, as we didn't even notice the love is there. We thought we are doing it to fill the void that we have, although sometimes we do things truthfully because of the loneliness. As time pass, the relationship went stronger just because we already gave the person a place in our lives. We become more open to each other, trusting each other gradually, and manage to get into deep discussion of different opinion. The circle then getting larger.<br />But it took a great effort to prove someone that they can trust us. Especially when they have painful history of being betrayed by the person they used to trust the most. It took real patience and time. We might not understand at the beginning but we will learn the truth as time passes.<br />This book also taught me that everyone has their own past that they regret, regret of doing, regret of not doing, and the heavy burden they carried with them since, causing the real sorrow and doom atmosphere, not knowing how can they repent those feelings that leave them being socially withdrawn. That some of people deciding on suicide would be the best way to end everything.<br />These feelings occur to almost everyone to some point of our lives, and somehow I easily drawn into this book from the beginning, because I know the feeling of looking up to someone that we can regard as mentor at the end, someone we call 'sensei', and our effort to show them how meaningful they are to us. <br />As the book went, I find it extremely easy to relate on the feelings of worrying on our family members' well-being, sickness, health and many others because they are important, wonderfully important people in our lives. We don't want to regret of not spending enough time with them, although they keep saying they are fine. Because family, they don't want trouble the people they love, especially of parents to children or vice versa.<br />This book splendidly brought up the theme of daily human interactions to each other in a handsome manner, that at the end I managed to say "the book couldn't be written any better and ended any better."</span>
  <a data-text-id="15216243973475276757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3498177755" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3498177755" />
    <a title="kaelan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4763420-kaelan"><img alt="kaelan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328812583p2/4763420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3498177755?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="kaelan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="kaelan" href="/user/show/4763420-kaelan">kaelan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3498177755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17449206685950365567"><i>
  <b>Heart of hearts...</b>
</i><br/><br/>Another one for the re-read pile! Like <i>Wuthering Heights</i> or Ferrante's <i>Neapolitan Novels</i>, Sōseki's <i>Kokoro</i> is all about the limits of inter-subjectivity, about how we seem fated to forever misinterpret each other (and ourselves). And since the novel abounds in subtle shifts in perspective—not only does the first-person POV flip about halfway through, but the characters themselves are constantly foreshadowing or (re)remembering past events through the lens of age and experien</span>
  <span id="freeText17449206685950365567" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>Heart of hearts...</b>
</i><br /><br />Another one for the re-read pile! Like <i>Wuthering Heights</i> or Ferrante's <i>Neapolitan Novels</i>, Sōseki's <i>Kokoro</i> is all about the limits of inter-subjectivity, about how we seem fated to forever misinterpret each other (and ourselves). And since the novel abounds in subtle shifts in perspective—not only does the first-person POV flip about halfway through, but the characters themselves are constantly foreshadowing or (re)remembering past events through the lens of age and experience—I expect that reading it a second time would be like reading a different book.<br /><br />I can't speak to the other English-language translations of this Japanese classic, but I was moved by McClellan's simple and unassumingly elegant prose.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17449206685950365567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2998884377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2998884377" />
    <a title="Darryl Suite" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5873473-darryl-suite"><img alt="Darryl Suite" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335028349p2/5873473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2998884377?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darryl Suite" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darryl Suite" href="/user/show/5873473-darryl-suite">Darryl Suite</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143106036" href="/book/show/7094813-kokoro">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2998884377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5995859930998436387">Is it too early to call this my favorite book of the year? Because right now, I feel like I have been blessed with transcendence. The quiet storm.<br/><br/>In 1910s Japan, an aimless young man finds a kindred spirit in Sensei, an older gentleman who happens to be a bit of a recluse. The young man is desperate for life lessons, and is hopeful that Sensei can provide him with existential answers. The book is separated into three sections: Part 1 explores the two men’s unexpected friendship. Part 2 is the na</span>
  <span id="freeText5995859930998436387" style="display:none">Is it too early to call this my favorite book of the year? Because right now, I feel like I have been blessed with transcendence. The quiet storm.<br /><br />In 1910s Japan, an aimless young man finds a kindred spirit in Sensei, an older gentleman who happens to be a bit of a recluse. The young man is desperate for life lessons, and is hopeful that Sensei can provide him with existential answers. The book is separated into three sections: Part 1 explores the two men’s unexpected friendship. Part 2 is the narrator’s return to his family home. And the third… well, there is a shift in the narrative voice, and this change becomes the heart, brain, blood, and soul of the novel.<br /><br />Kokoro examines how guilt and the things left unsaid can have ruinous ramifications on the outcome of someone’s future and state of mind; there are characters who stay quiet and complacent, and their acts of indecision ultimately leads to devastating consequences. Even though guilt and silence are universal themes, there were several times I found pieces of myself in this work. Notably, there is a very specific plot element that takes place, something that mirrored a situation I’ve recently gone through, and this sent me cascading down that pesky rabbit hole called grief.<br /><br />My physical reaction towards this book: I cried. Now, now, now, I am not ashamed to admit that I can get emotional to works of art. I can’t promise that Kokoro will resonate with you in the same way, but it did leave me emotionally bruised. Kokoro touches on the unfairness of life, the shattering of innocence, the disillusionment of broken promises, and the perils of regret. It’s subtle, quiet, melancholic, introspective… and it hurt.<br /><br />When I picked up Kokoro, I expected to love it, but I didn’t expect to fall this hard and this fast. I am now a Soseki convert and it doesn’t even matter that I only have this one book to back up my claim. Blessed, I tell you.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.instagram.com/p/B7pB7hYAlcK/?igshid=1wlqtbk06g19b">https://www.instagram.com/p/B7pB7hYAl...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5995859930998436387" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3292156713" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3292156713" />
    <a title="Andragel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40079336-andragel"><img alt="Andragel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459801961p2/40079336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3292156713?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andragel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andragel" href="/user/show/40079336-andragel">Andragel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143106036" href="/book/show/7094813-kokoro">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3292156713" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17531097703395289949">A book as delicate as a butterfly, its wings fluttering in the wind, spreading the fragrance of a fresh honeycomb. No wonder Haruki Murakami named this to be his favourite writer. Both have a way of glancing into the depths of one's soul and writing from the heart and this meditation kindly providing a profound insight into the nature of loneliness is amongst the rawest, most heartfelt I have ever read.<br/><br/>I have always been fascinated by the way in which Japanese writers are expressing their ideas,</span>
  <span id="freeText17531097703395289949" style="display:none">A book as delicate as a butterfly, its wings fluttering in the wind, spreading the fragrance of a fresh honeycomb. No wonder Haruki Murakami named this to be his favourite writer. Both have a way of glancing into the depths of one's soul and writing from the heart and this meditation kindly providing a profound insight into the nature of loneliness is amongst the rawest, most heartfelt I have ever read.<br /><br />I have always been fascinated by the way in which Japanese writers are expressing their ideas, with such fluidity and clarity of mind one could be easily deceived about the magnitude of such works. But beyond the clear waters lie the intricate webs of the human mind.<br /> <br />So is depicted the encounter between the three main characters of the book: the unnamed narrator, the mysterious Sensei and his friend, K. The book's thematic spectre is pervaded by this "ache of modernism" a man of his age was probably feeling at the dawn of a new era. The book effortlessly captures a remnant of the old Meiji one, as Soseki is bound to witness his country opening to a series of Western ideals championing egotistic individualism and the pursuit of self, in stark contrast with the ethical stance he presumably adhered to, and all of his characters standing at this impossible crossroad are bound to suffer without any fault of their own.<br /><br />There is little to say about the book which has not already been said over and over and over again. All I can add is that one would never ever be able to put into words the extent of the effect this book may have over one's soul. I have been presented its plot at large and warned about its devastating impact, and yet reading it was amongst the best decisions I could make, anyone could ever make, really. Even if someone were to hate Kokoro, it would still be a worthwhile experience to have, if only for wanting to find out if Soseki deserved his reputation as one of the finest authors this world would ever have.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17531097703395289949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/286663267" />
    <a title="Declan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5848260-declan"><img alt="Declan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1427664982p2/5848260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/286663267?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Declan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Declan" href="/user/show/5848260-declan">Declan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099339106" href="/book/show/1934039.Kokoro">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer286663267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4052995617684190656">This is a very quiet book, one that whispers its way through the details of a friendship between two men but which brings us to an understanding of how the understated detail can be more intense and painful than one which is expressed loudly and with force. The level of restraint and discipline displayed are admirable in many ways, but they coexist with an a great deal of hypocrisy and an expectation of self-imposed subservience .<br/><br/> However the book was marred ,for me, from the beginning by the fa</span>
  <span id="freeText4052995617684190656" style="display:none">This is a very quiet book, one that whispers its way through the details of a friendship between two men but which brings us to an understanding of how the understated detail can be more intense and painful than one which is expressed loudly and with force. The level of restraint and discipline displayed are admirable in many ways, but they coexist with an a great deal of hypocrisy and an expectation of self-imposed subservience .<br /><br /> However the book was marred ,for me, from the beginning by the fact that I could not understand what it was that had first attracted the narrator to Sensei, the main subject of the book. At first I thought it might be a sexual attraction ("I saw him...swim out all alone, I was suddenly overcome with the desire to follow him"). But as the story unfolded there seemed to be no basis for that theory, but neither is their any basis for the friendship which develops. Sensi comes across as overwhelmed by the sadness of his own life and his self-absorption leaves little mental or physical space for either a friend or for his wife who, within the narrative of the book, exist only as an adjunct to Sensei. All the narrator's conversations with her are about Sensei.<br /><br /> If you overlook that puzzling aspect of the book and allow that the details of the friendship are simply as they are presented in the book, it is a compelling and reflective novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4052995617684190656" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/698363693?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Clausen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Clausen" href="/user/show/957634-daniel-clausen">Daniel Clausen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer698363693" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12685889428357705524">私はその人を常に先生と呼んでいた。だからここでもただ先生と書くだけで本名は打ち明けない。<br/><br/>This is the first line of Kokoro, a fantastic line that sets the stage for the story of the relationship between a young man and an older gentleman he refers to as Sensei. <br/><br/>"I usually call the person "sensei." And so, here I will just refer to him as "sensei" without revealing his true name."<br/><br/>Like much of Soseki's books, the tone is gentle with quite a bit of dialogue and not much of what Westerners would call action. Many of the book's themes are univer</span>
  <span id="freeText12685889428357705524" style="display:none">私はその人を常に先生と呼んでいた。だからここでもただ先生と書くだけで本名は打ち明けない。<br /><br />This is the first line of Kokoro, a fantastic line that sets the stage for the story of the relationship between a young man and an older gentleman he refers to as Sensei. <br /><br />"I usually call the person "sensei." And so, here I will just refer to him as "sensei" without revealing his true name."<br /><br />Like much of Soseki's books, the tone is gentle with quite a bit of dialogue and not much of what Westerners would call action. Many of the book's themes are universal: existential dilemmas, the inability to make connections with others, the impossibility of living life without hurting others. Other themes are more related to the time of the book: the idea of suicide as an honorable action (for example) might not be readily understood by Western readers. Thus reading a good introduction to Natsume Soseki’s life and work might be a good idea. <br /><br />Also, a note for those trying to read the book in Japanese. Even though this book is an older book, published in 1914, the Japanese is very reasonable and a upper intermediate student could struggle through the book. Because the chapters are short (the novel was probably serialized in a newspaper or magazine) there are easy breaks for those who want to read in small portions. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12685889428357705524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_415890651" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/415890651" />
    <a title="Rosemary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9462235-rosemary"><img alt="Rosemary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336790844p2/9462235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/415890651?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rosemary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rosemary" href="/user/show/9462235-rosemary">Rosemary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer415890651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5040024711710519256">The first time I read Kokoro was in the early 1980s, when I first began living in Japan. In the intervening forty years, I find I have forgotten many of the details but not the feeling of the book. It is an exploration of loneliness, sadness, regret, and the transitoriness (and perhaps futility) of life. <br/><br/>A university student happens by chance to meet an older man, who he calls Sensei, at a summer beach resort and pursues an intellectual relationship with him. Sensei appears to be a scholar but h</span>
  <span id="freeText5040024711710519256" style="display:none">The first time I read Kokoro was in the early 1980s, when I first began living in Japan. In the intervening forty years, I find I have forgotten many of the details but not the feeling of the book. It is an exploration of loneliness, sadness, regret, and the transitoriness (and perhaps futility) of life. <br /><br />A university student happens by chance to meet an older man, who he calls Sensei, at a summer beach resort and pursues an intellectual relationship with him. Sensei appears to be a scholar but he does not teach or write, he lives comfortably but has no form of employment, he has a beautiful wife but no children. The only thing he does with any regularity is pay a monthly visit to a grave in a Tokyo cemetery. <br /><br />As the student gets to know Sensei better and tries to draw him out about his philosophy of life, he can tell that Sensei is holding something back, something that makes him cynical and critical of himself. Sensei drops hints about his reasons but only in the form of advice he gives to the student. <br /><br />During the course of the story, the Meiji Emperor dies, which causes everyone to mourn and feel that old Japanese ways are fading. The Emperor's funeral is followed by the shock of General Nogi's suicide. General Nogi, along with Admiral Togo, was a hero of the Russo-Japanese War. But, as a young officer, he had lost his banner to the enemy in the Satsuma Rebellion, when disaffected samurai had fought against the Emperor's forces. The Imperial Army prevailed, but Nogi carried a sense of shame from having lost the banner. He felt he must continue to serve the Emperor but once the Emperor was gone, he was free to take his own life and redeem his honor. <br /><br />Against this background we finally get the full story of Sensei's life, the guilt he is bearing, the uncertainty about his behavior, and why he seems unable to enjoy life. This story is imbued with sadness and regret. It's as though Soseki has taken the powerful beauty of a haiku and rendered it in prose form. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5040024711710519256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QTnJHiXZSt09Tf3jq4+vO6YxjVAIJzcnMOYlsH3bElm+xSCDTjrXcYZFhsWAZFmdG9tJlkx0j848rGVHpufQqQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('0LN8296Np63v8GwX6XLlYOakJyNAytO89zMzGuQvbjgvT5VGtW46AVT4FzHCmRPGW07j5QSZa1X7eXPtPxOsyA==')})
  }
//]]>

In consequence, he feels himself &quot;disqualified from being human&quot; (a literal translation of the Japanese title).\n\nDonald Keene, who translated this and Dazai\'s first novel, The Setting Sun, has said of the author\'s work: &quot;His world … suggests Chekhov or possibly postwar France, … but there is a Japanese sensibility in the choice and presentation of the material. A Dazai novel is at once immediately intelligible in Western terms and quite unlike any Western book.&quot; His writing is in some ways reminiscent of Rimbaud, while he himself has often been called a Osamu Dazai’s The Setting Sun takes this milieu as its background to tell the story of the decline of a minor aristocratic family.\n\nThe story is told through the eyes of Kazuko, the unmarried daughter of a widowed aristocrat. Her search for self meaning in a society devoid of use for her forms the crux of Dazai’s novel. It is a sad story, and structurally is a novel very much within the confines of the Japanese take on the novel in a way reminiscent of authors such as Nobel Prize winner Yasunari Kawabata – the social interactions are peripheral and understated, nuances must be drawn, and for readers more used to Western novelistic forms this comes across as being rather wishy-washy.\n\nKazuko’s mother falls ill, and due to their financial circumstances they are forced to take a cottage in the countryside. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10764967749704984204">Natsume Sōseki (夏目 漱石), born Natsume Kinnosuke (夏目 金之助), was a Japanese novelist. He is best known for his novels Kokoro, Botchan, I Am a Cat and his unfinished work Light and Darkness. He was also a scholar of British literature and composer of haiku, kanshi, and fairy tales. From 1984 until 2004, his portrait appeared on the front of the Japanese 1000 yen note. In Japan, he is often considered t</span>
  <span id="freeText10764967749704984204" style="display:none">Natsume Sōseki (夏目 漱石), born Natsume Kinnosuke (夏目 金之助), was a Japanese novelist. He is best known for his novels Kokoro, Botchan, I Am a Cat and his unfinished work Light and Darkness. He was also a scholar of British literature and composer of haiku, kanshi, and fairy tales. From 1984 until 2004, his portrait appeared on the front of the Japanese 1000 yen note. In Japan, he is often considered the greatest writer in modern Japanese history. He has had a profound effect on almost all important Japanese writers since.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10764967749704984204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
